Let's be honest - Sega is reason, why they had to soldout themselves. I mean Sega Japan - they ruined they own company, for bombing all Sega America ideas.
It's part if the brain games that retail companies play to convince people to spend more. From the music that plays to the layout of the store, to the position of a rack of fresh French bread, everything is designed to mess with you psychologically. The Sonic sounds are probably two-fold. One, they make you nostalogic, which releases seratonin, and they trigger the pleasure centers of the brain, which also causes a release of seratonin. That's the original intent of that sound - to trigger a reward response, making the actuon desireablrle, imcentivizing players to carry out the action that plays it. Thus, a convenience store can use that connection to incentivize you to buy more. Keep playing the sound. It makes you happy. You want to be happy, don't you?

The ideal that Sega secretly made this random POS software is just silly. I worked in a gas station that used one of those systems for a few years and I know that while it sounds alot like the Sonic sound it is a little different. Sega used the Yamaha YM2612 sound chip in the Genesis. That chip, and versions of that chip, have been used on hundreds of games consoles, toys, computers and musical keyboard even to this day. My Yamaha keyboard form the mid 90s to early 20s included a sound bank of hundreds of sound effect including sounds that similar to famous game sounds. A sound like the Sonic ring sound was included. It is my belief that sample is a recording from a Yamaha keyboard from around the mid to late 90s. Since the sample can from they keyboard  the they wouldn't have pay a licensing fee.

It‚Äôs a common misconception that people rob gas stations for money. Really they‚Äôre just trying to let Sonic out of the register.
In May 12th 2022, a Twitter user named eU Bear(BearUNLV) told Alpharad that "Gilbarco Veeder-Root and SEGA Sammy Holdings manufactured the cash registers, they programmed them in for a ring sound effect because it was unique sound for a cash register"

They did help develop the OS. There are actually several different "sonic" sounds associated with the OS, although most of them aren't used and everything except the ring-up sfx are heard during maintenance and managerial functions. Both of which have sounds disabled for security reasons, so you never actually hear them besides when the register is first being setup.  I didn't know this was a controversial topic. The patents and general designs and their contributors are all publicly available information if you know where to look. Lol.

Here's my late theory; Sega sells their IP to Sammy and Sammy in turn licenses it out to 3rd Party, including Chinese (and I'm assuming German? whatever Gilbarco is) manufactures who made a bunch of registers. Cool, understandable, but why the Sonic coin pickup sound effect? Psychology. They know that if they can get you to even think about rewards, they can turn shopping into even more of an addiction. Think about memberships and reward cards now, how they make you feel like you're in an "exclusive" club and how you feel like you're being "rewarded" for spending your money at a certain location. If they can trigger the monkey reward pleasure part of our brains while we check out, using sounds from a video game and pachinko machines, there stands a change that subconsciously they can bring customers back. It's so slight that you think about it for a split second and it settles in your subconscious, subtly, bringing you back to that location.   Also gas stations are a cult.

Here's a theory for you. So we all know that those sounds come from Sonic, specifically picking up a ring.  Now, what's the best business motto for any convenience store/gas station? The faster they can get customers in and out, the more profit, right?  Now what's a common catchphrase with Sonic? "Gotta go fast" ring any bells?   But that's just one layer of this theory. It also plays to the people who prefer older genres of video games, late 90s to early 2000s era.  By that measure, your target audience would sit around 21-30 years of age. Prime real estate for those nostalgia blows. You here that catchy tone, brings back fond memories and as a kid, you most likely are candy and drank soda, yes?  So you're getting this nostalgic ping and the first couple of times you decide you're thirsty so you get a fountain drink and you could use a bit of nostalgia in the realms of flavor too, so you pick up some good, old snickers bars. Just like the old days.  But this isn't over quite yet, no. Think about mice and how they can be trained to do certain actions to get a reward like food or stimulation.  This falls within that same vein, eventually you become conditioned to that nostalgia and you've been programmed to grab a few items, maybe it's paper towels, or windshield wiper fluid, or motor oil, or whatever. Because this place now invokes the thought process of "well I can get that at the gas station."  Whereas you'd normally buy those things from a supermarket or an auto store. It's a one stop shop, with that added bonus of that hit of nostalgia.   But hey, that's just a theory...  A business theory, thanks for reading!

Actually it's a lot simpler than you think. For multiple years SEGA and Nascar have been doing cross-promos between each other. It was back in 99' that they started to put the sounds on cash registers in Stop N' Go's for example, and other NASCAR promoted convenience stores. Like your other theory, it just stayed with the cashier OS and become pretty turn for the OS in the cash registers and you hear it quite commonly now.  But look into it a bit, there's been many promos between Sonic, SEGA and NASCAR over the years. It's rather weird and neat.

Hey nexpo, i know im a bit late to the party, but i wanted to throw some input in here. Galbarco does a lot more work regarding the actual gas equipment. they focus more on the actual pumps and the tank systems. The veeder root product (which is a hellish thing that i will forever hate as it screams like a banshee if anything goes wrong) is something that monitors the amount of gas left in the stations tanks as well as the temp, water content and a few other readings that i never got into as a tech. The main system that you will see in the actual stations will be a be Veriphone or NCR(this goes for a lot of groceries as well). Veriphone and NCR are typically in charge of the actual POS hardware but only sometimes have a hand in the software. Veriphone has their own systems, but can have other software run on them with little to no issue. NCR is a bit more tricky and as far as i have seen, they ten to run independent software. so the sound file would be spread a lot further than you would think making this even more of a wide spread theory. Especially since multiple parent companies who have multiple things like gas stations, grocery stores, and even some general stores, which all have completely different hardware and software, all with the same audio file.  but this is just what i have seen working at a few different companies in the US so things may vary outside of my scope.

I'm a month late, but... Behaviorism is a psychological school of thought created by John B Watson as a means of being able to (pretty accurately) determine how people will behave in certain circumstances and situations. He, along with B. F. Skinner later, essentially created the entire concept of commercialism as we know it today. One important factor in the concept of encouraging spending is familiarity, and when you own the rights to one of the most familiar sounds in video gaming history, incorporating it into daily consuming gives people that "warm and fuzzy" that will make them more inclined to go back and spend more money there later, whether it's consciously or subconsciously

The best explanation I can give regarding the sound effect is that either SEGA sampled the sound effect for the first Sonic game and it's been utilized ever since due to Sonic's main gimmick being speed, like a car, which would correlate to a gas station. It makes sense, as Sonic 2's Special Stage theme begins with a buildup that wouldn't be out of place in a racing game.  The second best explanation I can give is the opposite, SEGA associates Sonic with speed, and as such, cars, so a popular sound effect would probably be used somewhere.

I work for a retailer that uses the sound effect of Mario getting a coin in super Mario world on our handheld scanners. Its the first sound you hear when you start the game. It's a sound so ingrained in my mind that I immediately recognized it when it showed up on our scanners. We used to have bulky gun style scanners that used a windows based OS. In the last couple years we switched to cell phone style scanners that use android OS. The sound effect carried over and has been used about 10 years. Im sure Nintendo did not license it to us so my guess is that its just a joke or a shout out from one of our companies programmers.
Ok but what about Basketball games using Mario music

I would like to say one thing, since I researched this same topic a while back:  Sammy never got any IP's from Sega through the holding company. Sega Sammy Holdings is a share-to-share company and only a share-to-share company. Both Sega and Sammy still work independently from each other, with Sammy still developing their own pachinko machines, and Sega doing their own things. So now that I've just watched the video which I thoroughly enjoyed. I will say that I am somehwat doubtful that SEGASammy is involved in the Sonic ring sound effect. It is generally hard to copyright or license sound effects, particularly from that era. At their core, they are synthesizer generated tones which can be reproduced by anyone using a professional synthesizer with enough patience. So at that point, it would be like trying copyright A 440.    My thoughts are that retro game enthusiasts are reaching the age in which they can implement stuff like that in their creations. I've heard mario mushroom sound effect at an electric car recharging station. For one, I highly that Nintendo would ever allow such a thing, and if they became aware of it, they would sue that company out of existence.

The topic of sound / sample propagation throughout society is incredibly interesting to me.  See also: the Wilhelm scream, the Amen break.  Fascinating stuff.  Excellent video!  It might be worth the research (though I don't know how you would go about it) to see where Sonic team got the original "ring" sound effect.  If they had a synthesizer or other software or sample pack that had that "ring" as a usable effect or sample, it's possible the two developers (Sega, and Gilbarco) came to use that sound independently - by using the same hardware or software during development - and the "original" is on some old Roland or Nord synth, or "Random Cool Sound FX VIII" pack or similar.  A similar situation famously happened to the music group Blondie and the artist Gary Numan back in 1980 when they used the same synth with the same built-in rhythm as an intro to their hit songs ("Heart of Glass" and "Remind me to Smile", respectively.)  I also mentioned the Wilhelm scream, another instance of sound propagation by which a scream recorded during production of a 1951 movie made it into the sound library of Warner Bros., and was then used in hundreds and hundreds of productions as a "scream/terror" sound effect in future movies, television shows, and video games too.  Star Wars comes to mind as one of the most famous uses of the Wilhelm, but a quick glance at the Wikipedia article shows entries as recently as April 2020 with new usages of the Wilhelm scream.

To anyone wondering what the answer is: I looked into this a couple years ago after hearing the sound at a gas station in Canada, and the answer is simple. Sega bought licenses to a bunch of generic sound effects for use in Sonic, so they couldn't trademark the sound. This company must have done the same, and it's strange yet plausible that none of the employees in charge of picking the sound effects for the POS system know anything about Sonic... Or perhaps everyone knows except the guy who approved it, lol

It got to me, too. I used to work for a company that emulated the Genesys/Megadrive, Saturn, and Dreamcast. All 3 of these machines could make that sound. When I say "make" it, I don't mean just play a wave file, like these cash registers probably do; I mean that they are Yamaha YM2612 FM synthesizer decendents, which use 4 operators or more per channel. Any 4 operator FM synth should be able to make the Sonic ring sound.

I HAVE A THEORY:  Back in days of mechanical cash registers, the drawers had a bell on them.  This was to prevent theft - the boss / owner of a general store could listen for the bell and know when the drawer was being opened even if he didn't have an eye on the clerk.  Thus, if the bell was rung without a customer present, the store owner could inquire why the clerk opened the drawer. Possibly they were attempting theft?  Anyway, even as things modernized, the "bell" stuck around and the idiom "let me ring you up for those items" became a thing.  To "ring someone up" means typically to scan their items, or manually type them in and total the customers items to charge them.  I suspect that whoever made the POS systems searched for a "ring" sound on the early internet or maybe was a fan of sonic games, and used the "ring" sound effect.  Somehow, they correlated sonic ring, with cash register bell ring, even though sonics "rings" are literal hoops (rings) and would be considered more of a "chime" sound.  But the developers used the sonic-ring sound in place of a bell-ring sound.  I remember hearing the gas-station sonic ring long before dreamcast (1999), so I doubt sammy is related.  Maybe there was some licensing directly from Sega, or maybe, Sega didn't make the ring sound themselves. Maybe the "ring" sound came from some other collection of assets that both the POS developers and sega drew from, so they would both have a licesnse.  Either way, I remember being a small child in the early 90's and hearing the sonic ring at a gas station and thinking someone was playing sonic and getting excited. This was definitely before 1999.

Wait, did you not consider that the ring collecting sound from Sonic the Hedgehog wasn't created by Sega? It was most likely taken from a public domain/sound effect library, as were a lot of video game sound effects from that era (same reason you hear Doom sound effects in half of all PC games made around that time because they were all taking sound effects from open-source libraries). Just a hunch, but I think it would be worth looking in to.

I know why.  There were studies done on this (don't ask for sources, I just remember reading or watching it).  The sounds in video games create a sense of accomplishment and gratification.  I think the study was specifically with Sugar Crush that I am recalling.  The sounds trigger a subconcious thing in your mind that keeps your brain wanting more.  So, on a marketing aspect, satisfying sounds to a person's mind on check out, would create a positive experience to the consumer, and make them want to come back more, completely willing to pay convenient store prices for normal items you could get way cheaper elsewhere.  People are lazy, and we will pay to be lazier.  Invent a product that will make people lazy and happy, you will be a millionaire.

This is so fucking scary
The sound in question is using a simple saw wave one of the most basic waveforms for a computer system to make. so this was a common sound computers would make . look at early video games such as sonic and other Nintendo games they all use the same analog saw/sin wave sound. This is because audio processing chips were not advanced enough to synthesize complex waveforms . So that explains the actual sound. But what about the notes? Well the notes are E G C played in an ascending arpeggio. The thing is that these notes are actually the second inversion of the c major triad the most common chord in music. Do I think this has anything to do with sonic? Unfortunately , no I just think that due to the outdated technology and the common use of the c major triad an amazing coincidence was created
